ith this mingling of remembered passion and gently smiling detachment the Austrian writer Robert Musil, then turning cY when we read Musil, or Stefan Zweig, or the other witnesses of

Vienna at the turn of the century, fond nostalgia is inevitably haunted by the sense of a moment not merely past but irreparably destroyed? World War I did not in fct end the urban lie of Vienna, or  creative role in of the other Western capitals, the discontinuity brought by the war was lue.Te aed Epeor Fra Jos died  1 withn two yas his army, the centuries-old Hapsburg Monarchy, and the empire all ceased to b In  Auta-Hunay appead fo the map  Europe, V      A beyond recognition. In the same year four of the men most responsible for the cultural energy of turn-of-the-century Vienna also died: the architect Otto Wagner, the designer Koloman Moser, and the painters Gustav Klimt and Egon Schiele. Certainly Musil and his contemporaries could feel that in had disappeared—and that it could never be replacdin kind.

In retrospect, those who lived on realized that their vanished world had in fact always been one of ambiguities, undermined by disquieting fault lines. The empire itsel seemed to have been built from top to bottom on the lly cn.Fo t n  J c o te with an ambitious new middle class determined to operate free of church an thron By 1860 ty ad dh o a conn  wre wy p.I   a' p t en treaty was another unwieldy compromise, setting Budapest and Vienna as semiautonomous partners in a dual Austro-Hungarian empire that extended from the Veno  Rusa n mbrcelevennatnal roups ith alos as many different tongues.

Size meant instability, not strengh, for this ambiguous empire felt itself to eiho  sl—epridn collectieniy etermind y blo eoaphy r traditin Nohn n the bastardiz or o te conu a o the libl politin—fferd y solid contrweight to te nationalst selfassertion and the emotional intolerant longing for racial separation which set Germanic against Slavic factions at the end of the assembly in 1897 and govern by decree.

The one solid presence that seemed to hold everything together was the epeor himsel whose apparenty indefatigable longeviy the Viennese 1818190 e I  y the olln  ih en  Fr Jo ife as well as his domain. Yet as the autumn parades of 189s formed, when the thy ha p h t F J only son, Crown Prince Rudolf, the heir apparent, had committed suicide han illit ovr t the royl ung o n Mayerig in 18, and the Empress Elizabeth, the melancholy "Sissi," was assassinated by an anarchist ar  ;   n  Sa    ot stuffof local drama but of global tragedy.

In remembering Vienna, Musil and Zweig were haunted by this retrospective sense of ill-fated destiny. But when we read these authors today. our pain is doubled by our knowledge of the still worse fate that awaited them with the rie of Hitler and the diaspora of Viennese culture in the 130s a 194. Ineviably we e the  throgh the ohr, forehort t u ooy o a 1 e  o hin l darker apocalypse, frther intensifying the poinant luster of the epoh it ended. The double optic enlarges and exaggerates. Things that belonged to life everywhere in Europe at the turn of the century—fin-de-siecle worldwearns ad dou,hec exuberance d social nrt ascendant ni-Semitism—take on special portent in the case of Vienna.

Furthermore, the Nazi era and World War II cast a pall over the aca-GV me er 1. In ths sene, turoteceny Vienna ws trebl "o" line in the genealogy of modern culture. Studies on Sigmund Freud and the genesis of psychoanalytic theory, on the formation of the philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein; on the modernist composers Arnold Schonberg. Ann n Webe, Al Ber he  reald Gst Mahler, and on te plays, poems, and prose of writers such s Arthur Schnitzler, Hugo von Hofmannsthal, and Karl Kraus—all these paths have in recent years converged, producing a new fascination with the city these creators all shared with compatriots as diverse as the founding Zionist Th Ht  chcuAoH

Nowhere has this resurgence been more dramatic than in the reevaluation of Vienna's contributions to carly modern art. Histories of modern art and architecture conceived before the late 1960s made Pars the master BM and Egon Schiele's paintings. or of Otto Wagner's and Josef Hoffmann's vincial domain of what would politely be referred to as specialized tastes. Thee re the elements o folklore  more bluntly of cliche. Part Athens part Babylon, Vienna before World War I has become a modern archetype of a doomed society, in which brilliant achievements glowed in the gather-Her Broch, a Krab ig te n o the world."s The sentimental Vienna of another generation—the world of Johann Strauss's Blue Danbe, of the Vienna woods and Linzer torte-has been superseded by its modern equivalent: the world of Richard Strauss's Also sprach Zarathustra of enius and neurosis—a light opera rescripted by Spenger. It is worth noting that this constellation o ideas rose dramatically in American popular consciousness in the 1960s, when Klimt posters began appearing on every dormitory wal. Not only the proto-psychedelic sweetness of The Kis, but the whole romance of Vienna-heated sensuality amid swollen materialism, flowered idealism under looming guns-suited the temper of the time.

![](images/a18d2ec07fcff9cfd27335f5ecbde60bda16ad46bb865f705350ac6ce75d58ef.jpg)

Within the narrower field of the history of art, pre-World War I Vienna intertwined not only with each other, but with their historical, physical, and intellectual setting. The rich concentration of so many innovators in all of Viennese art have come not from scholars of moderm art, but from intellectual historians, joining the work of Klimt. Wagner, et al. to the conditions of Viennese politics or to the concerns of philosophers and poets

But the td or in ontext hav cnext oftonT ing the orthodox, Paris-first view of modern art's progress. And this contextualist vision of turn-of-the-century Vienna, for all that it has illumitally pleasing neatness that exagerates on the one hand and compresses on the other, leveling out profound differences of ambition and of achievement. TV l    p is in need of reexamination.

Unity was an essential part of the dream of the dead empire-impossible tlin te Gesauerthe tol wo o . Te n  o —the ideal of diverse artists bonded to each other and to the essential character of their time and locale Ths i in turn a dream dreamed within anoer that of a modern avant-garde art not homeless, but integrated into a real community. Klimt and Wagner and Loos thus become tablemates of Freud and Mahler and Wittgenstein at an imaginary coffechouse for a T coai th y  e lfille the Viennese artists we study

The al Vin Gesnr  t ealiz   dzzi temporary showpiece; but its limitations were ultimately stifling, in the preference for a perfect local harmony that excluded the incongruous vari-po all cn  o ocic cn n popular, academic, and political life of the last decades. It alo has this kind of limitation, in that the will to tie all Viennese developments most signifi-reposin  olat provicial. The son Vienne  ta faors proxiate contexs ovr arger one, and ediate ciationsovr enat old  li ih Fre  the excn say, Rodin—can share this risk.

One of the most important routes to progress in any intellectual endeavor is t placemen ottenin froe cntr o  cnn   arg the reestimation of undervalued traditions. In the history of art, we have recenty witnessd jut ths proce, in the powerful reconception of the origins and meanings of modernism brought about by a reconsideration of the Northern Romantic tradition. But this kind of shift needs to transform the center, rather than simply fragment the field, before we really et some where new. If the ne attenton iven to Vienna fnally reuls ony i nophile special interests—something important will have been missed. We have passed through a period of enthusiasm such as Musil remembered. when striking differences—of talent, of ambition, of achievement—-have been held suspended in the"shimerng siificance"of a ne visn of hn V No  ly  tl become clearer, some of the components are powerful and provocative, while others are shallow, conventional, and overvalued by association with stronger neighbors. The challenge increasingly becomes, not to defend the idea of the "gay apocalypse," but to disassemble it, to learn something more balanced and expansive from its elements.

n talking about early modern architecture, we often look at build-te pivs on whch hisy tre. But  Vienna the intert in batt are wag n the surfae A conc for fa sbol. and decoration—exactly those things the Viennese most aggressively claimed to have left in the past—was their special contribution to modernism.

Modernist polemics in Vienna had a particular bogeyman, the Ringstrasse. This broad boulevard circling the inner city was laid out over the of public and private buildings in  hodgepode of revial styl neo-Greck Parliament, neo-Gothic City Hal, neo-Baroque apartment "palaces," and so .Suh conucn coninto e a 180s ut y en e street had come to be detested. The eclectic fagades became a metaphor for the discredited liberal era that spawned them—the token of a worldview based on makeshift pragmatism without unifying ideal, content to pastche tha cea. vy fdooVi o t 0

Their polemics against the Ring generally derived from two credos. First. since te present epoh deserved  own unique, globaly conistent syle (the Vienna Secession's motto began "To the Age, Its Art"). the old traditions embalmed on the Ring had to be wholly swept away in order for the new to emerge. Second, the new style would not be added on to architeeture s a atter of taste, but interatedinto it by necessity. This Nutestil the style of usefulness or need, was to be determined by something outside selfconscious art-natural law, the patterns of craft, the demands of structural function-and achieve its form by revealing those origins with transparent ern form would emerge inevitably.?

Such arguments were to become a familiar aspect of modernism throughout Europe, but nowhere were they more out of tune with reality than in Vienna. It is not just that Viennee buildins lagged behind the eric; o     l p o F x ple, though the Ringstrasse was frequently denounced as antiquated, the real problem was that it was arriviste. Rather than embodyin traditin, it cheapened , debasin virtually al the great Wester styls in a way that y artt noere to  ut Jap  Byant. Te ifly

then lay not in escaping from, but in finding a fresh way back to, these abusd traditions. The recurrent search of Viennese architecture was not for a vacant amnesia, but for a valid Auglicha standoff, neoiated sttemnt,  in ths c a workin partnershibetween te new spi and that of an appropriate older model.

exact contemporaries and lifelong antagonists; two others, Otto W a fruitful collaborators. Though the battle between Loos and Hoffmann defines Viennese modernism after 1910, Wagner and Olbrich were the leaders at the turn of the century. Their unlikely coming-together-the union of a born-again technocrat and a youthful Romantic-produced the first modern architecture in Vienna.

In the mid-1890s, they were both at work on the mammoth double commission Wagner had won, for the regulation of the flood-prone Danube Canal (p. 5) and the installation of a new urban rail system, the Stadthahn This lattr project ws populist politrebuttal to the Ring

Of course, the Stadthaln stations were relatively unconstrained exercises in style. Working independently on unique buildings—Olbrich on the 1898 exchibition building for the Vienna Secession and Wagner on the 1902 comon r te Churc Lopd n he Sti lt 0-— thee architects faced more complex problems of making architecture that boh sncnentional  iclate nfilar menn. Working from widely different points of departure, neither arrived at a solution by the direct path—the natural correspondence of new forms and new purposes—they both claimed to follow.

It was simple enough, for example, to make the Secession building do what it should, in an appropriately modern way. The Secession was a confraternity in defection from the older artists' society, the Kunstlerhausgenossenschaft, that had held a virtual monopoly on art exhibitions in Vienna. The reel ro' proram called r more  better shows, andt aesthetic demanded harmony between art obects and surrounding interior environments. To facilitate the changing installation spectacles that would advertise this viewpoint, Olbrich (a founding member) gave them an interior of skylit, flexible space with movable walls.

![](images/9bc6e5b7e9f55b59c2209da452e05b1543c79334dc3abe39fa6359258995af80.jpg)

But it was a different problem to make the building say what it should. Originally Olbrich conceived it in the mold of contemporary exposition hl, wh baa hi pyons  locl ei r e purpo  aBte    aro derten compressed into Delphic solemnity, purposely opaque (p. 3). Reacting against the stereotyped codes of Ringstrasse historicism, Olbrich reached for something willfully llegible in terms of Western conventions, the Symbolist s  naticc f the l o feelingCyp th c efn o c  inst  oe was honored here.

Olbrich made the obligatory protestation that this form was found in inner necessity, rather than merely invented. He said he had no thought of style, only the desire "to hear the echo of my own sentiments, to see my warm feelins frozen in cold wal."His reference to artistic tradition was e d  "h  w w x pressed the kouros-like reserve of "a pure dignity that overcame and shook me as I stood alone before the unfinished temple of Segesa.

There was a wide consensus in the ninetis that architecture had to guide modern man to something better than the fragmented individualism of the i   l pt  cp  po lti. Hel bid ho hi e i wen, tn scity wold a cnt y n subjectivity but by intensifying it, in order to liberate primal energies. Wagner, though he supported his protege and the Secessin as a whole (he d in I), ws headed n a y different dicion. For hi, the n h    po ments of rational efficiency.

Particularly in his 1895 book Moderne Architektur, Wagner made his reputation as a modernist by espousing a ruthless disregard for anything but practicality: (His reaction to the empress's asassination, in 1898, was to po dat refor  terl cy  tht secat cul better and pallbearers would not have to turn corners with the coffin.)to He held that the primary focus of the architect should be on satisfying the funcional requirements of the building down to the smallt deil Materials should be chosen on criteria of cost and ease of maintenance, and structures should above all be simple and economical. Any thought of form would be a later,  utiael nneessay refinement, sie buildings made according to these pragmatic, reductive precepts would inevitably. Wagner asserted, be in a style appropriate to modern times. The cohesion of modern society would then come from what he called "uniformity raised to nuentality,"and the individual would be a happy cherin te mase.

![](images/5664f05807be90c985e2e5abe25128766c22ccf885df717ae105382eed6f18cb.jpg)

![](images/e70aebe20cad4d0bdc2189b2215ca4c8976f97cfc63baff5ff44c9916d6723b7.jpg)

The contrast between Olbrich's and Wagner's ideas of stylistic and social unity represents one of the classic oppositions of the epoch: between the utopia of the pulse and that of the piston; between those who would recover the spiritual, ritual bonding of a brotherhood, indicated by the term Gemeinschaft, and those who would follow the corporate model of cosmopolitan society, rational and secular, referred to as Gesellschaft. Not surpriigly, Olbrih longed boll for coniti o sall dwelgs. he le Vienna or e patronage of the Grand Duke of Hesse, at Darmstadt."

Wagner thus declared his distance from traditional building, not by simply abandoning its clements, but by expressing them in explicitly denatured f    H R   n   d evident his newly self-conscious, experimental, and transformative relation to them. More surprisingly. the modern elements he used as tradition's counterpoint often entailed equally self-conscious theatrics. Though the bolt ea n the ban' f ppody al tual nessit, th in fact decorative and symbolic as much as functional. The shafts of the bolts are not aluminum; the gleaming heads are only enlarged sheathing caps, added to strike the modern note that will be carried through in the aluminum-clad furniture, footings, and hardware inside the building

Just as in the church, such decisions about structures and materials were based on criteria of expression as much as those of economy: Wagner had used aluminum even more prominently in the 1902 façade of a news dispatch bureau, Die Zeit, where with its protruding rivets it made an ressivey ntraditonal sateent wol approrte to n ofe tht trafficked in contemporary events. Both the aluminum and the prominent riveting were carried over into the Postal Savings Bank, but there each was incorporated into a progra intended to express as wel a reassuring solidity continuiy. If the Postal Savings Bank does not square with Wagner's own laws for core of rational modernity. Wagner's modernism here is as much a matter of Wagner's motto was the chillingly no-nonsense Artis Sola Domina Necesstas ("Necessity Is Art's Only Master"); but in his Nutzstil, invention is more often the moter o necessity. More in  poety than n s praatics, W grand modern style.

Wagner went on to build more canonically modern buildings after the ban and the church—the severely unonamented apartment bloc at Neustiftgasse 40 and his second personal villa (p. 37) are among he best-known and most geometrically decisive of these-but he never had the chance l  t a were fustratd through the machinatons of enemis he had made alon his way. He was convinced for example that Archduke Franz Ferdinand. who had acidly slighted the Steinhof church at its dedication ("The style of Maria Theresia is still the best" was the royal nephew's sole comment on the occasion), was personally thwarting him  Wagner was more honored abroad—made president of the International Congress of Architects in 1908, for example-than he was at home. Yet, while one laments the injustice, the unbuilt buildings may be less regretted, since it is clear from the project schemes of the late years that his inspiration was running increasingly dry.

![](images/c0ffe88b5a01f8dc82df6e753aa450aa05d7c6d007ec019998514b71531af784.jpg)

Wagner was one of the last architects of his time to encompass with natural ease both the functionalist faith and its contrary the recognition that human needs were defined as well in terms of symbols-be they gold Baroque domes or bi aluminum bolt heads—that were neither functional proportions and with very different levels of energy and conviction. In the 1894 Nussdorf Lock (p. 27) he had audaciously and successfully let raw engineering and unapologetic symbolic ornament have their way in open both the tradition and the modernity became more attenuated, in his monumental, synthetic tours de force of the Postal Savings Bank and the Steinhof church. After 1910, when the traditional forms became desicated and the n  moey moe proraatc Wr ben o produc  r s appealing blend of dead classicism and totalitarian rationalism. In the "ideal metropolitan complex he conceived for an architectural gathering at Columb University in 19l1, bureaucracy and megalomania le down together to dream of never-ending, mediocre uniformity-airy, spacious, rational, and mind-numbing. The memory of Schinkel fades before the premonition of Albert Speer.

Wagner kept planning for yet another, postwar reformation of Vienna, right up to 1917. But his inner compass had gone askew, and the man who prided himself on the sharpest attention to mundane practicality had eviny ot tc h se ac ealAer e   15 ievin War ad begn  dailydirsic corrpondene wihhr. Some measure of his mental insulation may be gathered from the note he inscribed there eflectin on the conequenc o the sssatin oFra Ferdinan in Sarajevo: "I consider that the death of the Crown Prince has removed the greatest single obstacle to the practice and further development of modern architecture in Austria."14

![](images/c05cd6a9480c49f74e11217791a0eacf88cf3735dd28c98dabbc29d9b0e2171b.jpg)

W out him seemed untenable. Both Josef Hoffmann and Adolf Loos, for example, were great admirers of Wagner, but neither could sustain his blend of imperial classicism and industrial confidence. N ofinpration outside the established Wester traditions, and wound up marking off enemy sectors in the area of Ausgleich that had been the common ground of Vienna's first modern architecture.

Hoffmann and Loos were born within a day of each other in the same region of Moravia, but they charted opposite careers in Vienna. As Wagner's prize student and a founding member of the Secession, Hoffmann t      O for Darsta, he ws the favorie architect of the Secesion supporters, c ow toh om  le  o waly n.From his position as professor at the Kunstgewerbeschule (appointed 1899), and nese taste.

Loos was by contrast the perpetual loner, born to iritate rather than . He   Vin t a  A pa e ier fo te .A  faon h te Secon sn His ce s literay moe tan   h clost cte  te qly mordnt cit Vinno Kal Kra ulatin ras, who published a virtually one-man newspaper of opinion, Die Fackel ("The Torch"), Loos once briefy produced a magazine of his own, whose title pithly conveys his stance: it was called Dus Andere (The Other") and subtitled "A Publication to Bring Western Culture to Austria."

The temperamental and artistic differences between the two architects were car and histoy h tended to dra them even moe sharply. For all their differences, Hoffmann and Loos started out in roughly parallel directions, with shared admirations. Both looked to the anonymous products of English and American verracular as models for a modern form of their clients'-not to mention their own-taste for high refinement, and mous low style pulled on one side, a new sense of elitist individualism on the oher. Both men initiall st out to resole this tension by means of the same o     t   o Anglo-Saxon vernacular with the best of Greco-Roman inheritances. It was on what actually constituted the best in each of these instances, and on how the modern architect should effect the union., that Hoffmann and Loos disagreed absolutely.

Hoffmann was a devotee of John Ruskin and William Morris, and a strong admirer of contemporary English design that had links to the Arts and Crafts movement (C. R. Mackintosh, C. R. Ashbee, H. Baillie Scott., C.F.A. Voys  But thee inter were tied to larerbio. The cool, plain, geometric oganization of these dwellings on the Hohe Warte,n area then on the nV      y redctiv all-mbracin new tylosimplicity

![](images/b0ce72e7b1cb2345585e8fb0e204a674ae446cda225729d52e8be0ba3d8b335e.jpg)

![](images/4a60814bbc6b826a91b2e676d2d9c62106c8b4fc149b9d7eb54d85af1235465a.jpg)

The other part of the equation showed itself at about the same time, in his installation of the Secession's fourteenth exhibition, in the spring of 1902. This was the crowning achievement of the Secession's program for unified design, a collaboration of scenography with painting, sculpture, and music. to provide the proper setting for the Leipi sculptor Max Klinger's monument to Beethoven.17 Klinger had enthroned Beethoven in godlike classical nudity, on rocky heights above the eagle's reach, as a triumphant new Prometheus (see p. 155). Hoffmann's design in turn imposed a strict quadratc planarity of snow-white and old, to express the rarefed atmosphere of this celebration of Olympian genius. In sum and in detail (notably the remarkable overdoor reliefs composed exclusively of staggered rectangles) the design raised to featureless monumentality the rectilinear order announced on the Hohe Warte.

The Hohe Warte villas and the Beethoven installation together arise from specific aesthetic and social currents of the time. First. they represent Hoffmann's involvement in a broad wave of retrenchment that swept all of Europe in the carly 1900s. Rejecting the effulgence of Jugendsti's millennial abitions, ths new conservatism favored the bracing and monuental over the war and intiate, a sout probiy in a moe"oecti"ocabulay of sharply defined form, often explicitly classicist. But Hoffmann's particular inspirational models—English houses and Beethoven-—were Northern; his classicizing involved a Germanic mperative to distl cold essences fro the forms of the Mediterranean achievement.

Second, the villas and the exhibition also imply two visions of the artist as exemplar in the new order. The Hohe Warte colony speaks for the English view of a workman-artist's life, lived amid well-designed comforts and likeminded friends, while the celebration of Klinger's Beethoven honors the Germanic ideal of the uncompromising genius divorced from mundane humanity. The goal of a stylistic simplicity that would merge these two— allying Ruskin and Nietzsche-had a political background, in the attempt to reconcile collective socialism and more anarchic individualism. It also had a l   te ill t  te l l ol values and the absolutism of elitist order appear to share a common author-R H and purity drew on one of his generation's cultural heroes, the composer Richard Wagner, fr the Gesantunstwer ideal that empowered the artist to determine every object and aspect of life.

Loos, on the other hand, said no to all of this. Not an idealist by outlook. he cleary saw that the Wagnerin brand of sentimental idealism ws potentially oppressive. He wrote satirical attacks, transparently aimed at Hoffmann, on the tyranny of the total-desig architect,®d sought to el at ha xt o thap l W Ho t bl boda—eten the  beten  d lLoos wanted to draw more rigid distinctions, keeping the practical functions of bldiiue qun fro th

On virtually every point, Loos confronted the same choices and inspirations as Hoffmann, but acted from a wholly different perspective. He too, for example, looked to the commonplaces of the Anglo-Saxon tradition as models for a modern probity in design. But he was emphatically a city man, with no residual Arts and Crafts affections and no patience for ideals of latent grandeur in the rustic. What attracted him in anonymous vernacular was its sense of artless practicality and self-effacing reserve, and he found this spirit not just in quaint country houses but in the modem urban milieu

purity, while Loos's issued from pragmatic neutrality. The differences are evident in Loos's Cafe Museum of 1899 and Hoffmann's Purkersdorf Sanatorium of 1904-06.

The Cafe Museum was a cold blast of whiteness directly in the face of Jugendstil in 1899. Yet, though the bare look of the façade and the strictly uncluttered interior earned this place the nickname "Cafe Nihilismus," its order came not from simple negation but from recalling a Biedermeier tidiness of Austrian design of the 1830s, and from importing an Anglo-American spirit (one part of the cafe was hung with Charles Dana Gibson illustrations. The brass-and-wood decor, the bentwood furniture, and the seating pattern itself all drew on these proper urban-bourgeois sources, aiming for neither rural coziness nor aristocratic elegance, but impassive. "correct" civility.

As Loos's adaptation of existing models for the furniture and detailing reflected cynicism about design reform, so Hoffmann's outfitting of the Pur-- tere monumentality of the Purkersdorf building's conception, and the ssinonlit te de   whoiffen

In the glissando"30 of the Stoclet façade, the crisp rigor of Purkersdorf yielded to dreamy refinement. The ideal of classicism had abandoned the Northern, brute and rational, to comingle with the Eastern, precious and mystic. Whatever its starting point in contemporary English notions, ultimately the conception of the Palais Stoclet looked not to Anglo-Saxon vernacular, but to Oriental splendor. (The Greek goddess of wisdom, Pallas Athene, stood or the entrance to the house Ganesha, the elephant-headed Indin deity, was at the eastern end.)

![](images/63c85adb97c62c005aaa71f6cc1f00540a5337c70d91134d09b7bdaebbc794d5.jpg)

A new luxury is affrmed in the Stoclet interior (pp. 64-73). in unparalleled fashion. Given carte blanche by his wealthy young artloving client, Hoffmann here brought to bear a collaboration of the best decorative talents in his newy foundd design workshop, the Wiener Werksttte,and an extraordinary range of luxury materials. From the marquetry floors and specially loomed carpets through the kid-suede covering of the chairs and the variegated marble, onyx, ad malachite inlays of he walls gleamin beneath crystal chandeliers, this was a tour de force of symphonic orchestration (pp. .).Yet no gaudiness obtruded: all the richness of the inlays and innate patterns in the stones and woods was restricted to the smooth, flat planes of nobly proportioned and logically arranged sequences of spaces. Hoffmann's goal was to articulate an ideal of high luxury for a clientele repelled by the gross heaviness of the Ringstrasse generation's decor, just as historicism. Purkersdorf had been the requisite first step, the formation of a new basic vocabulary through which such enrichments, formerly denied, could then be restated.

Something changed, however, in the translation. The Palais Stoclet's opulence banished any trace of brash confidence in favor of a reticent interiority. While Athene stood for antique dignity outside the chaste façade, o So  t selembracing adolescent of Georges Minne, and Fernand Khnopf I Close My Door upon Myself (which was enshrined in an altarlike structure in the music room; p. 68). And the crowning image, the fabulous stone-andglass mosaic Gustav Klimt designed for the dining room, evoked an ecstatic ent t e d in te erardn tend l a (pp.70-73) When one imagines the Stoclets providing the final touch by carefully selecting the color of their evening accessories to coordinate with t eo.the n xclui ndu ho art is impeccably sealed.

Against such aestheticism, Loos set himself up as a modernist Savonarola, preaching ascetic reserve as the proper faith of modern man. His most notorious essay. "Omament and Crime" of 1908 (written with the Stoclet building in prore), mounted a barrae of moral eonoic, ad evolutionaycharges agaist decoration. Loos sawte death  tadtionalamen t cr, and the absence o appoate new dective sty s histcl inevitabilities, to be embraced rather than lamented. His was not simply an vanguard, to dress themselves and their lives in accordance with the English-tailored stoicism he saw as the most cultivated attitude toward the inevitabilities of history. Ornament was permissible for peasants who had no other pleasures, but modern urbanites with access to the fine arts had no ned of i. Such lines make wonderful reading, but are incomplete as guides to what and high classicism marked his development, as well as that of his nemesis. Ap he   M h  07 e a. Hoffmann's. In this vest-pocket interior, the Anglo-Americanism of the to V   o conscious classicizing reference (the coffered ceiling) and realized in more lh materil All th d especialy te space-extendin multpliastripped-down modular simplicity to expansive complexity.

More evidently with Hoffmann but just as certainly with Loos, the faith of reason and the seduction of extravagance played out a complex amour. Loos's attraction to bare smoothness was as much sensual s rational; he and his Spartan rhetoric is belied by deluxe designs that range from the quietly expensive elegance of tailor's shops, like the still intact Knite store (1909-1) on Vienna's Graben, to the Roman pomp of the remade Villa Karma in Switzerland (1904-06).35

Loos's infamous Haus am Michaclerplatz—the apartment block and store for the English-style tailoring firm, Goldman & Salatsch, that was his first commission for a complete building—is an almost diagrammatic example of the intersection of the reductive and the rich. Attention is usually focused on the astringent spareness of the stucco upper stories (and their implicit hui  the oae Ipl Resdene aros te suare). Ths part seems the most uncompromisingly modern, even though Loos protested that its plaster plainness was true to older, indigenous building types in Vienna. But what is more characteristic of Loos is the juxtaposition of this strict blankness with the lavishly veined stone of the lower, classically o t  x ofreferences, an English bay-window system).

Loos insisted that the heterogeneity of the Haus am Michaelerplatz facade—emphasized by the break between the lower, commercial and upper, residential parts—was the sign of its resolute urbanity, this kind of building, he argued, could exist only in a metropolis, where diverse func-I Loos would seem to follow Otto Wagner as confirmed boulevardier. But where Wagner thought of the big city's impersonality as happily homoge nized—"uniformity raised to monumentality" —Loos's vision was newly fented and by iplication alienate individual personalitis that shared the city space.37

Loos nonetheless had his own kind of sentiment, in constant tension with his haughty asceticism. All the Romantic emotion he excluded from buildings and objects poured with surplus into his heated vision of painting. He wanted from art a savage, confessional honesty that would compensate for its opposite, the stoic facelessness he prescribed for modern design. Clothes a buildings should be silent, but painigs should shri his greatest admiration went to Oskar Kokoschka.

Even within architecture, he drew a sharp distinction between the facade, which he felt must present a "dumb" face to the street; and the interior, where the wealth of individual personality, fantasy, and emotion culd have free rein His interiors thus show not onl a more sophisticated English fireplaces and inglenooks for hearthside coziness, and bedrooms suggestive of hothouse eroticism (his wife's own bedroom was carpeted with House (p. 51)-radically denuded, unconventional forms—should in this respect be compared with the floating marble planes of Hoffmann's Palais Stet. Ech htct n lene  dn cs o nor i but n wHo i  aS c L i ci l ern existence.

Loos's constructions were far from functinal simpliciy. On the contray, hs coplex ackin adinterockinonterir volues, adhs insitnce on specific luxury materials, posed terrible difficulties for contractors, and made the interiors potentially onerous to inhabit. Nor was he against tradition; he constantly stated his allegiance to inherited ways of Austrian building as well as to antiquity. Nonetheless his buildings. especially those of the teens, were sufficiently bereft of ornament and unfamiliar in form to attract the admiration of nascent functionalists, to his beneft and chagrin. In the cucil bding of the ar teen, sh  teSteinr Hoe  pecally the Sche House, Ls actualy see n portant respec to hav retacd his steps, back toward the flat-roofed, planar look of. for example. Hoffmann's Purkersdorf building of 1904-06. In carlier work he had not balked at the open show of classical elements, notably in the essentially ornamental columns of the Haus am Michaelerplatz and the Paestum-like Doric arcades of the Villa Karma. But in the Scheu House, he turned, as Hoffmann had much earlier, to the idea of deeper assimilation, and to the model  Meditrann vnacula—n th e, Aern terracho at    womo nt  ee North Africa

Hoffmann, meanwhile, moved in the opposite direction, away from plainness and toward overt historicism. In the Stoclet garden he enthroned a freestanding Doric column as a fountain (p. 46)—a token of his willingness to abandon the search for featureless synthess, and return to the legible l the Wiener Werkstatte's exhibition in Rome and the Villa Ast (both 1911) to the Austrian pavilion at the Deutsche Werkbund exhibition in Cologne d the umptuou Vil Skya-Prmave oh 114—hows the mer ence of a sharply mannered neo-Greck style. Radical, monumental siplicity-overscale, fat coluns without base or cpial eetin acue triangular pediments-combines with graphic geometrical repetition to produce Art Deco before its time. Here and in Hoffmann's further work of the teens, geometry is increasingly engaged in a punning play between abstraction and quotation—a development that has more and more to do with Cubism

This new, mannered classicism finds its converse in Hoffmann's increasi ffcin fr  o puquly  folk sye Te u cny house built in Moravia in 1913 for Oto Primavesi was the most impressive exp  th  atn h the acn ear homeland known s Hermatstil. The loglook exterior with its sharply pitched roof was matched on the inside by rooms busily decorated in flowered and geometric patterns borrowed from folk ornamenta style couched in the vocabulary of expressionism, with gaudy coloring and

Previously, both Hoffmann and Loos had dreamed of an architecture of unity, whose simplicity would join classicism and the vernacular, and whose appearance would be an integral expression of the new, modern spirit of its a iTe   t i  R h pp These ideas of unity had corresponded to larger intellectual and political aspirations in Austria. The desire to merge high, rational style with rougher oc  opoli prorpld  e ord Zivilatin h the al ldpl  the word Kult A the wih that a return of the Ringstrasse. The splitting up of Hoffmann's style into mannered urban rationalism on the one hand, and emotive volkisch decor on the , sues not simply the retun to histoict clectici, t the n   tc the notion of the state. And Loos'sinsistence on the blank uncommunicating façade, divorced from interior life, was not just a simple regression to costuing It radically reformulated the casual hypocrisies of the Ring strase's disjunction between private ad public expression, as the most bitter of modern truths.

Moser's firsthand knowledge of diverse craft skills and his experience with commercial art (from hs cary work s n illustrator) made him a leader in the Secesion. He shaped the look of Ver Sacrun and imposed a xb allatin tt a providential match with Hoffmann's more rigid architectural logic From their first collaborations in the Secession shows until they parted ways in 10t h  e i o o o  V rative arts.

Working o harmoniously that t is often difficult to distingush one man's work from the other's, Moser and Hoffmann were primarily responsible for the black-and-white geometric style that banished the cozy curves and wood modern desin. One need only compare the lacquered stool Hoffmann designed for Moser's studio in 1898 with the small cubic table he made for the painter Carl Molls villa in 1904 to measure the change. The interior cabinetry of Hoffmann's 1902 villa for Moser on the Hohe Warte—fur announcements.

Looking back on the changes in Viennese design, one critic said in 1908 that "the main event" of the preceding years had been "the conquest of applied art by architecture." This new tectonic rigor came from the vocabulary of modular, geometric desin that Hoffmann, Moser, and like-minded collague worked ut ond 1902-04-n lpurpos vabulay that ependin n sale,had the bility to ive small fors monuentality(pp. 120, 121) and major constructions an almost precious lightness. The absolut of eomety could ive brute, cubic authority to a chair's form, and at the same time—repeated  patte on the surface-dissolve  bul into o    e nant form was the impersonal and scaleless square, and the most pervasive ee t   cheerd   te ah pap

The fact that rectilinear symmetry and square motifs had already appeared in Secessin design of the ate 180s ds no diminish the si ndis. Much  been ade  Brih fns, crny Hoffmann's admiration for the work of Ashbee, Mackintosh, and others is indisputable. The bony, monochrome rectilinearity of Mackintosh's Glasl  t Britsh desiers brought with them fro the cold island Bu, aide 1 the siplicy Hoffann d Moser wrought in the objecs was more eciiv, and mo ody n.

The new Viennese reductiveness reflected a reimagining of the lessons of plainness in Japanese design, in opposition to the gaudier 1890s japonisme of France, and an insight that such distant and exotic models were in harony with hubl thin tat y close to hand. Tse local models included siple fors of homespun, which is hardly surprising. But they also included. in a way that seems more peculiar to Austria, the middle-class yl of the Vormar period (literally, "before March," meaning before the revolution of 1848), the derivative of neoclassicism known as Biedermeier.

The decades prior to 1845, dominated by Metternich's efforts to restore a pre-Napoleonic order, have often been derided as a low ebb of creative imagination in Central Europe, an interlude of garden-tending burgher complacency. But for Hoffmann and Moser, and others of their eneration, this semed to have been a specl moment in thehistoy o midlecass tste, r te omnatn ote stcc  bef th erro ohitm In the plain fagades of its buildings, and in the unadorned shapes of its generation and now to be recovered. The Biedermeier style's sedate authority offered just the remedy, too, for those withdrawing from the more anarchic ambitions of Jugendstil.

For Hoffmann and Moser the challenge from the outset was to recover richness from reduction-to rephrase simplicity with a reticent elegance that would render superfluous the conventional luxuriance they were elimina Evn in th sa y, t we endin ndn was to cheape noen atel d l fahyhanr podue no th wod   pl— rather than merely deprived. Classic instances are their characteristic basework andhammered-etal ct (pp.120-3) Without disturbin the in onent nitand bo  eadi decati ch ment. The impulse behind such design reform was after all not asceticism, n al historicist bourgeois taste.

It was hardly coincidental that hammered- and punched-metal objects were the hallmark early productions of the artists' design collaborative Hoffmann and Moser founded in 1903, the Wiener Werkstatte (Viennese Workshop. For the Werkstatte, this dialogue between e simple and the soisticated ws a matter of busness s much as aesthetics, oe of t prmay oals ws t tanslate the innate moral strengh  ood des and craft into commercial appeal. The working manifesto Hoffmann and Moser Wer   e  t l with matters of product value.

Yet the manifesto exhorted as it advertised, and was as much sermon as sales pitch. Appealing to the sons and daughters of the Ringstrasse generation to give back to middle-class taste its progressive force, the authors modern bourgeoisie. What was at issue was the ideal that avant garde values and middle-class values should be partners, not enemies. The found. ing premise of the Werkstatte was that at its best, the bourgeois spirit— h  cbiation o peueafi ordins d plied remain confused with crass, sensual complacency, but could be s forward looking, as refined, and as disciplined as these clarified new objects of enjoyment. It was in this spirit that Hoffmann and Moser urged patrons to ad to treaue nsted te deepr qualits o conception  fabrcation they would offer. In jewelry, for example, they pleaded that well-wrought sr nd miprecs stons houlduthine ld a dimond n the t teonnr (pp.10-4.

The Werkstatte itself, however, never succeeded in establishing the broad clientele or effecting the widespread design reform initially envidIn l   oal  te  e  to  to e lon maTe woe deavor   ma posib ro te nderwritng neosity of Fr Warndoer, the Anglophile ion of wealthy textile-manufacturing family who was a patron of Mackintosh as well as Hoffmann. His initial financial support allowed Hoffmann and Moser to design exemplary working premises, gather the best talents, and late to-quality  materal nd equipen. As h the des workshops of the Arts and Crafts movement on which it was partly modeled (notably C. R. Ashbee's in London and Mackintosh's in Glasgow), this operation was intended to be self sustaining, but that would have required extraordinary commercial success and very good management, neither of which was acheved. Overspending and litigated debt were chronic probsupporters.

Eventually even Warndorfer's deep pockets were emptied, and the enter prise came to be a shareholding arrangement with a board of directors made up of major clients. In this fashion, the Wiener Werkstatte continued, and even flourished, long after its founders had lost their creative roles and its products had changed beyond recognition; it finally closed only in 1932.s

This interest in folk culture, which was connected to the larger phe nomenon of "primitivism" in Europe, also had broader counterparts in An cWih teain niel l 0 t cas por  e cnrativ  appan Fran Ferdnand who was resoltly nti-Britsh nd ntiFrench), te ural t   o ea .o '    o anti-urban polemics of leading writers, Provinzkunst (art drawn from, and devoted to, the provinces) was ascendant.

As volkisch admirations intertwined with nationalism, local and particular truths were valued over so-called universal ideals. Thus the conception of the heroic and epic, previously centered on the iconic reserve of Attic i t   No  O e o piv of the an ork coneid y Win Wetat  for children was the illustrated Die Nibelungen made in 1908 by Kokoschka's professor at the Kunstgewerbeschule, Carl Otto Czeschka, for the publisher Gerlach's Youth Library (p. 131). Yet both books exemplify the fascination with fairy tales, enchantment, and folk myth that, from the edges of Europe (Scandinavia and Russia especially). came in the early 1900s to challenge Westerm art's traditional basis in Mediterranean culture (and captured, among others, the Russian painter Kandinsky in his Munich years). 0

There had been a split within the Secession in 1905, and the core of original leaders had followed Gustav Klimt in departing This had left the most progressive wing of Viennese art without any regular exhibitin space. collaboation of the "Klimt Grou nd he Wiener Werksatte shaped the giant production, which included Moser's installation of a major Klimt c    t   oy H complete advertisement Viennese artists ever mounted for the notion that artistic design should shape every corner of life, and also the announcement —in the savagery of Kokoschka's talent, which here first reached a broad ing endeavor.2

In a room whose walls were accented by vertical panels of lacelike linear amentation, the Werkstatte diply featrd amon ohr ojcs of decorous elegance, the flowered ceramics and fruit-bearing cherubs of Powolny (p. 136). The center of the room was given over to Czeschka's incredible wrought silver showcase, two years in the making The domi-Romantic, eclectic individualism brought to the fore. For several of the Wert e   M fashionable clientele. In the Kunstschau extravaganza, between the thin, mannered elegance of the wall decor and the chubby charm of Powolny's gaily colored Baroque-revival putti, one can measure the justice of his judgment.

Czeschka's work, too, pointed in a problematic direction. But from the advent of Kokoschka onward, the new and influential talents were essentially atectonic and pictorial; and the Werkstatte's p abandoned, in favor of more sculptural and often more precious formal inentions, andmore complex playall-consuming patter (.137).

The loss of tectonic logic bespoke the demise of the early reform spirit of the Werkstatte enterprise. The chic frivolity that characterized the Fedr to nrati  hnableic al so.Te Kucha wa a an son. It mared the edon ch that had begun with the founding of the Secession, an epoch in which the decorative arts had been central to all that was progressive and ambitious in the modern movement. After 1908, these minor arts once again became minor, responding to taste more than shaping change, and the Wiener Werkstatte became, more and more, simply a business built on embellishment.

he years following the 1908 Kunstschau saw the Werkstatte extend international reach, by exhibitions in Rome (19l1) and Cologe p   D We (founded 1907). But a severe retrenchment followed, as World that was recognizably Germanic in character, without French or English tait. This provincialism had its strongest effect on the newest of the Werkstatte's departments (and one of its most successful commercial endeavors), the fashion department, founded in 1910.24

Fashion and art had intertwined earlier in Vienna, in the friendship between Klimt and Emilie Floge (p. 198). Floge, who with her sister ran a leading Viennese fashion salon (whose quarters were designed by the Wiener Werkstatte), had around the turn of the century designed gowns so c  e   t ultimately responsible for them. She had also amassed an impressive collection of peasant lacework and embroidery from the eastern provinces and Rumania, and incorporated fragments of these directly into her designs well before folk style was fashionable. But more important, her early works n        p

At the end of the nineteenth century, the issue of women's clothing involved the larger issues of feminism. The fashionable silhouette of the bustled and corseted ladies of the 1880s was regarded in progressive circles y body in smock-like fullness that permitted freedom of movement. This kind of garb, known as the Reform dress, was for a long while the exclusive u pecc rouns. The breakthrogh came in mergig te plain, straight lines of the Reform dress with a redefined classicism, either in Empire revival noter instance of Biedermeierpoch resurgence) or in mor boldly e d In th a "natal e  the t cly  p  w     k at  been considered styleless and marginal for decades was repackaged, in the early years of the century, as the latet development f taste—the so-called Rational dress of Floge and Klimt.26

The next step leading toward an official Wiener Werkstatte fashion undertaking came with the establishment of a textile workshop in 1905. Hoffmann and Moser had already worked successfully with the textile firm Bachan Son  carp d oly bt t  the -o operation to focus on more lavish hand-printed and painted silk designs.27 With such luxuy cloh, manfactured n literal thousans o patterns cnay plenh y the Werate' adn Hofan Czeschka, et al.Vienna first made its mark on the interational fashion scene.

Hoffmann had always regarded textiles as an essential part of the total cient's clohn culd dtr th cplee harony tat propted the foundin e hin department. Bu tho Hofma contbted the founding director, Eduard Josef Wimmer Wisgrill. Wimmer Wisgrill was regarded as the Viennese answer to Paul Poiret, and a review of his h    a. th cpln uhas ua bo e Wer.

Wimmer-Wisgril's and the Werkstatte's outstanding success in Germany and Austria-to take nothing away from innate talent or quality of execution—owed much to the social need to beat Paris at its own best game. Nowhere was Paris's dominance more overbearing than in fashion design. and thus nowhere was it more imperative-or more difficult-to respond to

n balance, it would be diffcult to call the Werkstätte a success in the terms it st for itself. Citing the ideals of John Ruskin and William Morris, the founders envisioned an alliance between collaborative artists dedicated to the notion that handwork was

morally fulfilling, and an educated bourgeois clientele that would value enduring quality over fickle fashion. Through this allnce the talents of the best designers would be supported in their desire to produce functional al      .T Werkstte on prodctin, howr, never ealy estalish el n mass-market basis. While the best work was reserved for lavish, one-of-a-c  a  S te n  ll  te tiv. The l  reforing te lie of te coon man thro better design remained a paper promise.

Unfortunately, the very nature of the Werkstatte's ideals partially doomed its heritage. Its founders intended their work to be seen in contrapuntal ensembles, with materials and styles played off against each other in balanced interiors. With the one extraordinary exception of the Palais Stoclet (still remarkably preserved, in excellent condition), nothing now survives but the fragmentary components of that intention. The temptation, then, is to choose sides and forge outside alliances: either to isolate the geometric work and insist upon its proto-Bauhaus severity and functional-ly postmodern, in the fashion of the historicizing design or of the pattern-anddecoration painting of recent years. But in the best productions of the Werkstatte, the strict simplicity and luxuriant complexity were complementary, not opposed, principles—ways of emptying out and filling up that wol bnhgaiy  def o sen matl ple

matters of medium or style. Yet Klimt's ascendancy in the first years of the twentieth century has often been noted with reservations. The eroticism and elegance of his art seem guilty pleasures of mere glamor, allegedly redeemed only by their being rooted in the dark soil of Freudian impulsion. and by Klimt's engagement with such morbidly primal human subjects as aging, death, and-the quintessential Viennese affliction-anxiety. Kokoschka and Schiele have just as often been portrayed as modern aveng ing angels, for stripping away the gilded refinements of Klimt's world to expose deeper truths, painful and raw.

This vision of Viennese painting, even though it accords with the pronouncements of some Viennese artists and critics themselves, seems inadequate on several counts. It implies prior assumptions that sensual pleasure on its sleeve. Such an account slights some of Klimt's most personal and coherent work while elevating more earnest but less original effrts, and tends to miss the complex strategies in expressionism, far less direct than the straightforward baring of the soul. It may in fact be a reversed view—of i cyn lal et n exp m tell e n m tc te mt creative achievements. As in Viennese architecture in which the separate screen of the façade was crucial to modern expression, so in Viennese paintin, insight and overlay, matters of depth and matters o surf, were complexly intertwined.

![](images/bb4d2093160696540567c93d12ba4abe8dce595a92bcd2edb8db83102210bdd6.jpg)

t the turn of the century, Klimt embodied both authority and rebellion. Born in 1862 as a goldsmith's son, he became a professional decorative artist in his teens, and in one instance executor for the designs of the "prince" of Viennese art in the Ringstrasse era, Hans Makart (p. 150). After Makart's death Klimt, regarded as his t bdinAs n honod yu proesnal tu cade cial respect, in the 1890s, from forward-looking artists' clubs like the Sibener the Seven o Josef Hofmann nd Koloman Moser. The first two university paintings, Philosophy and Medicine, were not particularly original conceptions, and the shock they caused in 1900-03 is more a measure of Vienna's artistic provinciality than of Klimt's creative daringThe device f  nonspecifc allegory, based on a tangl of nudes oall ages, had become common coin in late Symbolism, and the crepuscular vision of humanity proffered by Phulosophy and Medicine, with self-absorbed elsewhere in Europe by the sunshine and vigor of vitalism.

![](images/7cbbd4879981e001ef4874b83f1695e2e012c2a00bca158edcd0845f1f60aa7b.jpg)

One of the most evident sources for Klimt's overall imagery and individual figures dates back to the 188os: Rodin's Gates of Hel. Where Rodin's figures in the Gates are driven from within, spasmodically thrusting and clutching in every direction, the crowds in Klimt's Philosophy Medice ft uad ie moltn forn ios supenson. Other -e-cenuy , h  t Noegi sulptor Guta Vige land, similarly constructed pillars of humanity from Rodin's model, but we drawny const, t exaerate s alre sen physicaliy In  B Junt nd e neri nt  Rod ferers—s disavowed. The realism was more trenchant, the nude bodies characterized in bony specificity, while the abstraction was at the same time more insistent: the chiaroscuro fog of Philosophy had been pac aki ymaA onalcin bn ppland te s Ba prpci  te indefinite. The image's severity, reinforced by a palette of black and gold T D que vision set remote and unfeeling law high above a punishment scene whose protagonists, a sagging old man and three sinewy women with the from which innocence was no defense (p. 153).i1

Yet for all the effort toward portentous gloom, there is something unconvincing about this conceit. The repulsive yet decorative polyp and the stylish demonism of the knobby-kneed studio harpies have their own odd n th nor e h ll  a  wl embodiments of grand ideas. Here, with the weightless poetry of the earlier allegoris banished, we are more agressively aware of the clumsiness of Klimt's self-consciously earnest symbolic apparatus.

Klimt doubtless felt this kind of monumental expression should be his highest goal. A frustration with what were seen as the limitations of easel painting similarly goaded other ambitious European painters around 1900, lonng for art to take on transcendent themes in rander fors. It remains n  evlai fe b   ce th whea M Gn and Signac among them—few succeeded in the way they had hoped. Klimt was nonetheless drawn not just by the hazard of commisions but indepenento the notin that by weddin il to chitecture in a new w could work in the nobler atmosphere of philosophy.

Klimt's most elaborate testament to such ambitions is the large frieze he painted on plaster (al seco) for the 1902 Secession exhibition organized around Max Klinger's monument to Beethoven (p. 4). Klinger's sculpture h   p o ele   e el n of suffering and salvation appropriate to Beethoven as redeeming Ubermensch. In the adjacent side hall, Klimt—apparently drawing heavily on Richard Wagner's exegesis of Beethoven's Ninth Symphony—set out a cycle Ho (Debauchery, Excess, Sickness, Madness, and others, on the front wall), while the longings of mankind fly over these to find solace in Poetry—all n o  co  n  c

A reverent mythology, and an accompanying hagiographic literature, has grown up around this "legendary" frieze (only recently restored and returned to view after decades of inaccessibility). But in all the ink concd t e necph  ylmo  wh Klit to e meni  e wor te  en suriy it cal coent  mo problematc pec. Te bas poa pause; the man in armor now appears as much Fuhrer as white knight (an Hoffmann's installation desin, p. 4. Moreover, the expression of the idea e to  short  her e re e pro enddTe p ed ape and his cohorts project more camp extravagance than evil, and the angelic choir seems thinly smug rather than exultant. What is more to be because it represents Klimt's retreat from the public ambitions of the university allegories, into a vision of art as solace, and sexuality as enclosed and globally consuming? Or because it embodies his persistence in the attempt to force lesser means-the melded visions of illustrative talents such as Felicien Rops, Aubrey Beardsley, and Jan Toorop, for example—to yield a monumental painting of programmatic sublimity?

![](images/4578548cd149a07170f1b9bee08d07da3f1e6e48555888ef5972da2664d81c83.jpg)

![](images/a372e9723b7dc899a8b7e5234efe4412f1a27eec3b198ae14b9d5ff6900c97e5.jpg)

![](images/06ad5310c684d32974260f786cb25fcc35aa64fd78103e5babc0d559d238a916.jpg)

Gustav Klimt. Adele BlocheBauer I (detail) 1907. d on canvas, 54% ×54%(38 × 138 cm) over all Osterichische Galeri Vienna

The strained seriousness of the Beethoven Frieze, and of Jurisprudence. might arguably be seen as a regression, drily and coldly against the grain of the unsettling erotic languor that emerged as a personal note in Klimt's art figures such as Rodin and Munch, Klimt found a way to put his finger squarely on a certain nerve in the Viennese, and indeed the larger human, pyce—in an area of fantasy where the desires of lust and luxury mingle pi A  n lcoy    T dit in musky, droopy-lidded lassitude, strikes an orinal d cplex note. But when he constrained this vision to express certainties rather than ambiguities—when Evil and Good and Life and Art were at issue—the music went tinny and the poetry cloyed.

Klimt's imagination came alive not on the chilly heights of idealism, but in watery depths—less among the Nibelungen than among the nympheas. As so often at the e of the centuy, the prial was conceivdin the first the thickets of parallel trunks in Klt's forest paintings (. 1) sugested Holer's notn o ojective order in nature, th nderwater c linarity,drifti into nd t e frmedfel, becae Klit's metpor for the subjective world, submerged in sensuality. The extreme instance of this vision is the Water Serpents of 1904-07, a parchment miniature of x of organic and inorganic life.

The Water Serpents demonstrates how Klimt brought into powerful concert specific naturalism and expressive distortion, spatial illusion and elaborate surface ornament. The bodies of the lesbian lovers are confusingly app o  d ne ed s or te t bd d silverpoint modeling of body forms, in conjunction with the vividness of fl o  patt, c te a to illat co in ue ground reversals. This kind of confusion and ornamental richness does not embellish the content of Klimt's art, it is the content. For Klimt the maintaining of irresolution—-between figure and ground, flatness and depth, object and y ay  ein t exprn   It oe t i  beni t o   . They also drew on a repertoire of ancient and exotic decorative motifs to assemble a vocabulary oUrfor o eoy nd bilogypirals, rectangle lozenes, trig nature19

These are the same combined goals—an anti-illusionist play directly on the viewer's sensorium, and an abstract formal language attuned to universal expression—that led other European artists in these same years to decisive certaints of sharp reductin d synthess. In Klimt they ave ie to elaboration and ambiguity. While others looked to sources in archaic and exotic art for a new economy of volume and line, Klimt saw in the same sources the heightened splendor of complexity—not only the blunt empiricism of a head by Giotto, but that head and its flat golden halo together, not only the woodcut simplicity of Hokusai's form, but that simplification overprinted with multipatterned kimono forms; not the white purity of Greek art at Segesta, but the rich ornament of the recent Mycenn fns. For hi, te exot, archai, d pimitive arts bore vin  l   ilbn sity of earthy observation joined with fertile abstract invention and lust for materials. This was the common ground that, from nomadic metalwork to Byzantine mosaics, shaped a tradition of spiritually charged art he sought to recover.

We might in this regard consider the best-known image in Klimt's decorated manner, The Kis, in contrast with Brancusi's roughly contemporary version of the same theme. Both artists were steeped in enthusiasm for o  o pp e we ffi e poSybol evain e ro Kl con to the goomy and danerous sexuality o his wors arond 100 (or exaple, Judith I: p. 189), here sought to embody a newly positive faith in the po lBu e Branoiz e osi in te abrupt ad vigros ters of caricature, Klimt envisond ther - even as it overloaded sensuality. The extravagant ornamental style was integrally a part of the play between certainty and uncertainty, opposition T   p    id richness of the formal means. Only in the harder, more assertively abstract S o ulnt   o that rivaled Matisse without replicating him, and opened up a fantasy domain of zoomorphic geometry later to be explored by Klee. Though evidently the stuff of dreams, the golden style was first, and in several instances most impressively, tied to portraiture. In the later period, which began roughly with the Kunstschau of 1908, Klimt's work fell off in intensity. Within Vienna., younger men with a very different vision began to assert themselves; and ' markedly.

In landscapes he returned to an early interest in the spaceless play of fields of broken-stroke pattern, loosely derived from Neo-Impressionism. In Viennese painting around 1900, a Divisionist tendency had been a feature both of moody Symbolism (pp. 190, 191) and of experiments in nascent abstraction.

These flattened, often horizonless quilts of flecked color were vacation work, done primariy dur ing Klimt's summers on the Attersee. The Gerstl-Schönberg episode yielded a small and problematic body of art that was tangentially connected to fully developed Viennese Expressionism, by sociological similarities in the supporting milieu and by common sources. Gerstl was perhaps te st painter in Vienna to register the ipact of the exhibi   Vcn  Goh  Sec n 0 the Galerie Miethke in 1906) and Edvard Munch (at the Secession in 1904). This tendency to divorce sitters from mundane surroundings, in order to focus on character alone, is also evident in the portraits Egon Schiele made around 1910. Emerging from under the benign but smothering influence of Klimt, Schiele stripped away the decorative surroundings that marked his first portrait attempts.3 But in place of the teeming fullness of Kokoschka's tangled atmospheres, he set figures such as Dr. Erwin von Graff (p. 168) and Eduard Kosmack (p 208) in blank vacuums, so that the energy of their gestures was compressed rather than radiated outward. The metaphors that commonly surround the discussion of this new expressinist portraiture are those of surgery:stripping away disguiss, probin into private verit. Where Klimt's art would be alleed in these ters to have been concerned with superficial appearances, the expressionist ision s said to lay truth barethe joint tuth, both of oher ndividuals' inner states mercilessly exposed and of the artist's own psyche invested without mediation in every act of art-making

But this account does not seem to fit the pictures. Far from defining the uniqueness of each of their individual sitters, the best eary portraits made by both Kokoschka and Schiele seem highly consistent, bearing a strong states of being, as a foil against which individual idiosyncrasies appear. One of Kokoschka's foremost biographers long ago saw that his portrait vision nel t o ny ce e c ed the features of his contemporaries; his tendency, she observed, was "to transform familiar individuals into fundamental human types . . always searching for the common factor in the human beings he came across."32 T l t  w a new, global sense of both the inner essence and the outer expression of a modern human condition: an inner life of disturbed energies not accessible tconola  eparate exterr e o concus unncu es that communicated through performance.

It hardly seems coincidental that Kokoschka's first oil portrait was of an actor (The Trance Actor, 1908), and that one of Schiele's closest associations was with a mime (Erwin Dom Osen, or Mime van Osen, as he called himself. Both artists amplifed the activity of gesture, even to the point of pl i   y  neal Teny pely te han, participatd n th portrat charcerizaton. Ther subjects slump, twist, grasp, and gesticulate semaphorically, acting out their loss and self-consciousness, rather than simply addressing the viewer in a p  eol sate o beiTe b sa of e cba moe thn the surgeon's chamber, seems the proper home of this vision.

It would be wrong, however, to draw too direet a line from Schiele's that a vast gap existed between the strange and frenetically disturbed Schiele we seem to se in his rt and the well-reed d relatively unprepossessing young man they knew in life Nor willit do to fall back on ody .Th s te e o exp area where it would seem most appropriate—in the unique series of nude behind obvious disguises and symbolic surrogates—strategies he shared not o with Kokoscka but with many oher moden artists—Schiele here did to play out an identity acknowledged to be acted as much as experienced. Both Schiele's and Kokoschka's works are theatrical But Schiele's most arresting self-portraiture is more specifically a theater of the marionette. exacerbating our sense of it. Schiele took the very forms that had been the pl — ae the   a n n  Vin ier ie fo sl concation  h d n neapble modern condition.

Schiele portrayed himself in two different aspects of the marionette. On te     exaat ho  po i well beyond the normal tradition of artists studying their own extreme Osn Schie ss to have sought a n alphat o nieral stal comunication, based on pedoscentif study of the pathology of human movements and expressions.46 and to have exaggerated these unconveno e o te    xpi ont th the experimen omoden dance in the r tentith centy.

On the other hand, in many of these images Schiele portrays himself inert and expressionless, literally a dummy, with blank eyes and disjointedly mechanical poses (pp. 148, 213). The same button-eyed, doll-like flaccidity I another world—that of the mannequins which became familiar personae, in the work of de Chirico among others, around World War I. The uncanny resists defining by formula, and it is hard to spell out the strategies by which Kubin succeeded: erotic deformation, incongruities in scale, and eerie nocturnal lighting all play their part within the general fabrc of uncofortably speciic rendering. Klemens Brosch had a far shorter career than Kubin's (committing suicide at thirty-two, in 1926), and has remained virtually unknown outside Linz. where he was born, and Vienna, where he studied at the Academy of Fine Arts  Like Kubin, he was a prodigy who began producing accomplished. personal work quite suddenl, s a teenager. Their source, to, are not dissimilar; Goya greatly affected Brosch's bitterly ironic scenes of World War I, Kinr's bae, harpy ece real prds all h y But Brosch drew with a clear-eyed sense of daylight dreaming wholly distinct from Kubin's phantasmagoria

What sets Brosch apart is his cinematic vision: a combination of mobile perpeci,  relens d  japste yy  s hat seems to link the world of Caspar David Friedrich with that of Alfred l together the mundane and random specifiecs of near and far into scenes whose emptiness suggests an absent narrative. Schiele had what Klimt did not: a virtually unfailing ability to command the sculptural complexity of anatomy twisted and forea an te  o rali o  h e pr His line seems relentlessly specific, and tied to lived experience, as it tracks rich decorative sense. Followed through the drawings, the line breaks and i   o occasions for color and pattern that lend racy stylization to his charac teristically sullen figures. Similarly, the emaciated bodies splay themselves across the pages in ways that suggest a mobile, never-complacent viewpoint and a constant resistance to an enclosing volume of proximate space. In all thee respects, Schiele's poers as n oserver are interally linked to is instincts as a designer.

It no longer dwells on the disruptive energies of youth or adolescence as challenges to established culture. Instead Schiele turned to procreation as a primal metaphor of continuity, a way of imagining the legacy of one generation to another. Klimt's early works on the related theme of motherhood had been haunted by fin-de-siecle forebodings of death-in-life, and the loss of self in te ol  .Th p n Si' pc   Ro more troubling. Huddled in mutual disregard in the cold darkness of an empty studio, these three, and especially the artist in his primal, simian crouch, have a numbed passivity that betrays neither fear nor hope. In a year of death, this iagination of brth raised, yt lft barrenly open, the Schil oud not i  n. What e e? Wha our legacy?

In a more confident and happier time, in I898, Koloman Moser had incorpoated into his huge sained lass windo for the Secesin bildin (p.5, a motto born of joy in the present, not fear of transience: "The Artist Shows His World of Beauty, Born with Him, That Never Was Before nor Ever Will Be Again." The artists who passed under that window believed that their community could make its own world, and that historical change was their natural ally. But in 1918, time's scythe cut with its other edge—Klimt, Wagner, Moser, Schiele himself, and the empire all belonged oy before te paint a drid nThe Fam What then  the world this community envisioned, of the beauty that was born with them? The questions Schiele seems to pose are still ours to answer: What of legacy? What was bequeathed to us by Vienna?

It seems crucial that we ask these questions specifically with regard to the M tists, and raised social issues of enormous consequence. Many of these cities a ha potant arts d cti Secessin movments. T ask seriously of Viennese early modern art the question, What is the le   py to  wc e  ,   y se went wrong

One underlying problem in Vienna, put in admittedly crude and schematic terms, was that of the balance between plain and fancy Many writers have seen that this balance was not maintained, but have disagreed on the reasons and the consequences. An orthodox modernist account elevates the moments of plainness-the sober, rational, geometric style of Hoffmann and Moser around 1902-04, for example—and bemoans fancy frippery as a fatal Viennese disease. Only Loos survives as a true hero to this vision, accompanied in an ambiguous way by the other enemies of aestheticism. Koa d Schi. On t oh hand  pe t h posoe ts  at te ncs Vin w  pru lar j—th ecorative ichness of Kli,and the eclect oamentalism of the later Wiener Werkstatte—and that this fun was suppressed by more dour, canonical international modernism. Neither account does much justice to either side of the balance, or sheds much light on what pushed the equilibrium askew.

![](images/8f6992b227b773e52f00377c6a1eb5999542ebd8a4a4006dffa063b1a6dfacf9.jpg)

Fancy Vienna, for example, was a lot more than just fun. Poised on the edge where Europe met the East, the Viennese had a special feel for Oriental splendor, not only in high Byzantine lavishness, but also in the intricate exuberance of Slavic folk design. In Moser and Klimt, in Czeschka and lgt  e patt. But t delecatn  the  w   prt Vinn mind in a crucial way. A new estimation of the decorative arts was key to ergent mode ie ohuman nature andhistoy.

Two key aspects of early modernism were entwined with the study of decorative arts: the assertion of the mind's powers, as above the biological laws that had dominated much of nineteenth-century thinking about man; and the disavowal of Greco-Roman antiquity and the High Renaissance as te   .In t 0 hum repreenati cam t more for what they revealed about the mind than for what they showed of nate, aent cae to be regarded s equal r superior to furativet as an index of a society. The major pioneer in this regard was the Viennese art historian Alois Riegl., whose work on late Roman decorative arts was down, but also of turning notions of culture around-by insisting that anonymous, unselfconscious patterns were the surest markers of an epoch's T ti lcati  R  e dc h i    t of Klimt, Czeschka, and others for "decadent ornament as a way out of naturalism.?

What seems still more characteristic of Vienna, though, is not just the lo o nt, te    nbe patter. Beynd the pleasrable sense of dislocation a dreamy suspenhas noted in discussig Klt, te conditon of readi fure and round xp e  an Mo   ccl o  l study of perception that became known as Gestalt psychology: Psycholothe fors of man's experience were not grounded exclusively either in e   t considered irreducible in any account of our relation to the world. As with te case o Rig nKli  it Moserd Getlt theory: t Vinne o radical developments in the modern rethinking of the human mind and of

Thi attaction  ectiv patt int  ffen. On te one hand, the perceptal confusion o dens orament could be seen l   t o G  It  tht haono natalim  teRenassance ws shallowherea e tnged horror tacui of Gothic and Celtc decorative patterning the kind that attracted Czeschka particularly) was characteristic of more spiritually exa  in wh ma' ill pe epd  t n world denying antinaturalist fors.

On the other hand, for many Viennese artists, especially after 1908. dn decatin sat n fectn r te a Baroe Ro, t e dn t environment into a heightened, more oceanic aesthetic experience (p. 99). Decoration w t r moe laboate sensoy stulation, not pt inner anxiety

The basic problem was not only that these two positions were opposed. but that neither restrained is dream of the fancy with some saving anchor in the plain. Each made a virtue of excess, which pushed Czeschka's talent into ponderous Gothicism, while it set some of the work of Hoffmann. I

Klimt's Stoclet mosaic, there was the promise of a powerful intrusion of Eastern splendor into Western art, aiming for the kind of synthesis between decorative pattern and a feel for the natural world that produced many of Matisse's most potent early works. But viewed as a whole, and especially 1908, theVienne love fr te fancy is ten driy, heartey fivo lous; its later achievements seem to belong to a lesser strain of modernism. more in tune with the world of Dufy.

The fate of the plain in Vienna is as complex as this history of the fancy. and shows similar bifurcation. Unadorned simplicity started out around 1902 as an ideal of a high order, platonic and world-affirming at the same time. Hoffmann and Moser started producing what they boasted of as e Moel (sple uie"), n the oher spae eoei c o the Wiener Werkstatte, in order to increase the sum of human pleasure. They thought that the spiritual rewards of proud individual craft labor could be harmonized with the sensual requirements of an elite new middle class, to the literal profit of all involved. The lightened touch of decoration and the clarity of geometry were intended to open up a newly refined form of pleasure in material life.

This simplicity was not sustained,as the Werkstatte'clients soon tireof the noble notion of good basic designs for all, and began to demand more obvious forms of luxuriance to suit their sense of privilege. The kind of plainne that did endure in Vienna Adol Loos' was altoetheriffrent. His insistence on bannin aent ws n  ior-eversl o Klt's lo o ecn. L o loe to e  e t sbols n the main doors of h Vill Karma), but primarily r leons in austere simplicity (as from Japanese houses) and for a certain strain of philosophy. Hs fatalistic sense of history and his ironic diffidence were in h wth a world-denyn h content with his sene o a mandan uperity to the tawy mesinss o coon li e how  for the war in a specially tailored uniform).7

He also explicitly affirmed what Klimt apparently understood intuitively, tht art ws nally, at  srs in en, dry ked to te erotic.8 But he would have none of the dreamy confusion that Klimt courted, and his engagement with science was nearly the opposite of the Gestalt challenge to the stability and constancy of experence. Lie his friend Karl Kraus, in Kraus's restrictive view of language, Loos had a sense o te pla that ws invold wih s reductioni  the delineation of hard boundaries. These men prided themselves on drawing polar distinc-d xiblh i  that could t be od n cryAs All Jani d Sten Tol poid L' tt h  crn t wh te s of Wittgenstein to circumscribe a reduced field for philosophy.t0 they had perhaps even more in comon with the selsty scienti spt of I a militant sterility was advanced as the proper condition of modern expression.

Now, again, the problem is not just that the two forms of the plain were irreconcilable, but that neither one came to any satisfactory or enduring engagement with the fancy. The Wiener Werkstätte's early simplicity had no base in what later would have seemed natural to its forms, a true functionalist aesthetic or one focused on machine manufacture; it either crubled under appled decoration, or inverted itself from reductive to
